For me, overcoming the 2007 season where he was named MVP and then eliminated by the \u201cWe Believe\u201d Warriors in the first round was when he exorcised his demons.  Shaun Powell :  How could it not be the wrong-legged jumper? Sure, there\u2019s much more to Dirk\u2019s legacy than that, including the manner in which he put the Mavericks (and Mark Cuban) on the map, how he spent his entire career with one team, his series-changing finger roll against the Miami Heat in Game 4 that helped win a championship, and being the finest European import the game has ever seen. Still, that jumper, that form \u2026 John Schuhmann :  He was nothing like we\u2019ve ever seen before, a seven-footer with incredible touch out beyond the 3-point line. He will leave a legacy beyond the numbers and the 2011 championship, because the one-legged, turnaround fadeaway that so many players have tried to emulate will forever be known as Dirk Nowitzki\u2019s shot. It\u2019s unguardable, especially when he (as he so often does) gets a pick-and-roll switch onto a guard at the nail (middle of the foul line), something I\u2019ll always call \u201cthe Dirk play.\u201d Personally, I\u2019ll also appreciate him for his attitude. There are some players in this league that take themselves too seriously. Dirk is definitely not one of them.  Sekou Smith :  I had the good fortune of drawing Mavericks duty for their entire playoff run the year Dallas won its lone championship in 2011 . I\u2019ll always remember how Dirk showed up time after time to lead the team, doing what all of the all-time great champions tend to do during title runs. The coolest part about it was just how unassuming he and the Mavericks were during that postseason run \u2026 well, save for Shawn Marion and Jason Terry (who regularly let the media know they were the team to beat). They finished off the Oklahoma City Thunder in five games in the conference finals and vanquished the Miami Heat\u2019s Big 3 in The Finals . Being on that ride, the best of Nowitzki\u2019s Hall of Fame career, was as fun a time as you can have. And watching an all-time great at his zenith, it doesn\u2019t get much better than that. "},{"id":47449,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/boston-celtics-kyrie-irving-marcus-morris-could-return-vs-toronto-raptors","date":"2018-02-06","title":"Kyrie Irving, Marcus Morris expected to return tonight vs. Toronto Raptors","content":" The Boston Celtics continue to set the pace in the Eastern Conference, much as they have all season long. In 37 games this season, Dellavedova is averaging 4.4 points and 3.8 assists in 19.0 minutes. Milwaukee\u2019s depth at guard will be tested as Dellavedova joins reigning Kia Rookie of the Year Malcolm Brogdon on the injured list. On Monday, the Bucks traded guard Rashad Vaughn and a protected second-round pick to the Nets for center Tyler Zeller. The Bucks next game is against the New York Knicks tonight ( 7:30 ET, NBA League Pass )."},{"id":41923,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/philadelphia-76ers-new-jersey-nets-crazy-game-trade-1978-79-season","date":"2018-02-06","title":"In 1978-79 season, deal like no other happened at NBA trade deadline","content":"Some NBA trade deadline deals in the past were seismic in name and impact and helped a few teams win or reach The Finals. There was Rasheed Wallace being sent from the Atlanta Hawks to the Detroit Pistons in 2004, Pau Gasol going from the Memphis Grizzlies to the Los Angeles Lakers in \u201907 and Clyde Drexler going from the Portland Trail Blazers to the Houston Rockets in 1995. Don\u2019t forget about Dikembe Mutombo going from the Atlanta Hawks to the Philadelphia 76ers in \u201801, Shaquille O\u2019Neal sent by the Miami Heat to the Phoenix Suns in \u201908 and last year, DeMarcus Cousins heading to the New Orleans Pelicans right after the All-Star Game. All beat the February deadline buzzer with a bang. And then there was the one on Feb. 7, 1979 between the Sixers and then-New Jersey Nets : Harvey Catchings, Ralph Simpson and cash swapped for Eric Money and Al Skinner. A minor deal? Perhaps, and yet: It was the deadline deal that threw the league for a loop, caused some temporary panic and created one of the craziest scenes in NBA history. It ensured that three of those players would play for both of those teams \u2026 in the same game. A game that needed four months to finish. * Box score:  Sixers vs. Nets game from Nov. 8, 1978 And maybe longer to completely comprehend, especially when you read the box score, which is confusing and needs to be preserved as proof that this indeed took place. The absurdity of it all is we\u2019re more likely to see someone beat Wilt Chamberlain\u2019s 100-point game before three traded players get some burn on both benches again. \u201cAll these years later,\u201d said Simpson, \u201cI still can\u2019t believe it happened. But it did. I know. I was there.\u201d To answer your first question: No, the trade didn\u2019t happen at halftime, causing the players to run down the hall and to the other locker room and switch jerseys. That would\u2019ve been crazy (and against league rules). But this is borderline crazy. This happened in a league you wouldn\u2019t recognize today. The NBA in the late 1970s was, shall we say, a different and distinctly quieter one than today\u2019s. This was pre-ESPN and Twitter and NBA 2K and \u201cInside The NBA\u201d. Teams traveled on regular planes, with players sitting next to regular people. Four NBA cities from then (San Diego, Seattle, Kansas City and East Rutherford\/New Jersey) are no longer around. The NBA Finals was shown on tape-delay after midnight because the networks \u2014 who are now shelling out $2 billion for rights \u2014 would rather stick with re-runs on Sunday afternoons and sitcoms in prime-time back then.  And so, there was nothing special initially about a Nov. 8, 1978 tip-off between the Nets and Sixers , played just a few weeks into the 1978-79 season. The Sixers had Julius Erving but the supporting cast wasn\u2019t yet ripe enough and Philadelphia was four seasons from its first of several Finals runs in the \u201870s and early \u201880s. Meanwhile, the Nets had sold Erving to the Sixers after the 1976 ABA-NBA merger and were barreling fast toward irrelevancy because of it. The former two-time ABA champs wouldn\u2019t taste respectability again until Jason Kidd arrived (in a trade) three decades later . The Nets did have a young, future Hall of Famer in Bernard King, though, and this is where the twisted tale begins. King was in his second season and wound up averaging 21.6 points per game that season, demanding double-teams nightly. The Sixers threw help on King all night and in the third quarter, the extra defensive layer was a hulking center named Steve Mix (whose biggest contribution to basketball was being big). King drove hard to the basket, there was contact with Mix, and King was whistled for an offensive foul. Enraged, King screamed at referee Roger McCann, ignoring the fact that he already had a technical. Now he had two and an automatic ejection. King kept screaming and gesturing and then punted a courtside chair on his way to the locker room. Watching this unfold, and reaching for his whistle, was the other referee, Richie Powers. Powers was one of a handful of referees who had clout and a measure of respect in that era, along with Earl Strom, Norm Drucker and Jake O\u2019Donnell. Famously short-fused and thin-skinned, officials of that era took no lip from players and coaches and often punctuated violation calls with colorful body language. Powers, a 23-season NBA official, worked 25 Finals games in his career. But this was late in his career, when the body begins to creak and the pace of the game gets foggier to the eyes. Just two years earlier, he was pivotal in the epic triple-overtime Game 5 of the 1976 NBA Finals, which featured legendary shot-making from Gar Heard and John Havlicek. (In that game, Powers was at the center of a crucial call. With the score tied at regulation and seconds left, Paul Silas called for a timeout in which the Celtics didn\u2019t have. Powers ignored it, rather than blow a whistle and call an automatic technical. That made Suns GM Jerry Colangelo say: \u201cIf it wasn\u2019t for Richie Powers, we would\u2019ve won that game and maybe the championship.\u201d) Powers called a technical on King, giving him three for the game and one over the limit. Nets coach Kevin Loughery jumped from the bench and raced onto the floor. He got three technicals from Powers, too, which was illegal. \u201cI liked Richie, but he had a habit of giving technicals,\u201d Loughery said recently. \u201cAnd unfortunately for me, I got a lot of technicals. That\u2019s one thing I regret in my career. They\u2019re human beings and make mistakes. Sometimes my reactions made it personal.\u201d The referees hated doing Loughery\u2019s games because he relentlessly worked them. He was usually among the most T\u2019d-up coaches every season and in his ABA days, he got six techs in one game . \u201cThat call on Bernard set everything in motion, then Kevin went ballistic and next thing I know, the game was out of control with all the technicals,\u201d said Catchings. The Nets played on without King and Loughery. Taking over as coach was the lead assistant, Phil Jackson; this would be his first stint in a head-coaching capacity. Taking over the scoring load from King was Eric Money, a point guard who originally scored 37 points, a season-high, for a game that went double-overtime before the Sixers won 137-133. Game over. Or maybe not. The Nets protested and the league office wasn\u2019t thrilled with Powers for his avalanche of technicals. Commissioner Larry O\u2019Brien suspended Powers for five games for \u201cfailure to comply with league procedure.\u201d * Box score:  Sixers, Nets replay game on March 23, 1979  O\u2019Brien also ordered the game to be replayed starting at the 5:50 mark of the third quarter, when the technicals flew. Given the demands of the schedule, the replay couldn\u2019t happen until March 23 , when the teams had a previously-scheduled game in Philly. Therefore, it would be a double-header: The makeup game first, followed by the scheduled game. Both teams agreed . But before that double-header, they agreed on something else: A trade between them, that Feb. 7, before the deadline. Catchings, Simpson and cash from the Sixers to the Nets for Money and Skinner. Some introductions are in order: Money was a shifty guard who averaged 18.6 ppg for his hometown Detroit Pistons in \u201977-78 , but played only six seasons for three teams. Skinner was a college and pro teammate of Erving but largely a journeyman whose biggest contribution to basketball came later as Boston College\u2019s coach. Catchings was a gangly 6-foot-9 forward who didn\u2019t begin playing basketball until he was 16 and wasn\u2019t recruited by any big program out of high school. Simpson was a big scorer in the ABA with the Nuggets, where he averaged 27.4 ppg one season, but never averaged more than 6.1 ppg in his NBA days . (Catchings and Simpson eventually became far less famous than their daughters: Tameka Catchings was a WNBA champion and MVP, while India Arie has won four Grammys and sold 10 million records. Also on that Sixers team were fellow teammates \u2013 Joe Bryant, Doug Collins and Henry Bibby \u2014 who produced famous sons Kobe Bryant, Chris Collins and Mike Bibby, respectively.) \u201cI welcomed the trade,\u201d said Catchings. \u201cI told Billy Cunningham, my coach on the Sixers whom I had a tremendous respect for, that if I stayed there, my career\u2019s going to be cut short. They were committed to Darryl Dawkins and Caldwell Jones. The deck was stacked against me. I wasn\u2019t angry or anything, but I told Billy if he could get me out of there, I\u2019d appreciate it.\u201d Simpson said: \u201cI didn\u2019t think I was going to get traded because I\u2019d been playing well. But I wasn\u2019t really upset. Harvey and I had become pretty good friends and I also knew Kevin Loughery from the ABA. We all had respect for one another.\u201d That trade raised an obvious issue: The suspended game. Once again, the league huddled, then decided the traded players could play in the makeup game, but for their new teams. And so, on the afternoon of March 23, the game clock at tipoff read 5:50 instead of 12:00, and it was the third quarter instead of the first, and the Sixers were up 84-81. Catchings, Money and Simpson played for the other team this time while Skinner rode the bench for the Sixers just as he rode the bench for the Nets in the previous game. \u201cThe strangest situation I\u2019ve ever been in,\u201d said Simpson. \u201cI was laughing about it, Julius (Erving) was laughing. It was sort of funny for everybody.\u201d  Powers didn\u2019t ref the makeup game. And nobody earned a technical, either. However, there was the matter of the official box score, which showed that Powers, in fact, did work the \u201cgame,\u201d as did three other referees. \u201cThat\u2019s when it really hit me, when I walked on the court and saw all of my old teammates,\u201d said Catchings. \u201cI thought no way would they let us do this, but then you see the clock and the guys you spent 4 1\/2 years with now on the other side, it was mind blowing. Dawkins was teasing me about it the whole game. You know how Darryl is.\u201d Money lost points in the process. Remember, when King was ejected, Money\u2019s number was called and he also had the benefit of two OT periods. But officially, he was credited with 23 points, down from his now-erased 37. He was the only one of the three who scored in the makeup game, getting four points for his new team, the Sixers, meaning he scored for both teams in the same game. The Nets lost that November game and lost this one too, 123-117. The teams went to their locker rooms, rested and showered, then put on fresh uniforms and prepared to play the second game of the double-header. The Sixers won again, which means they beat the Nets three times which counted as two. \u201cAll the fans got their money\u2019s worth,\u201d said Loughery. \u201cTwo games for the price of one.\u201d Technically, it was five quarters plus 5 minutes and 50 seconds worth of basketball, but at that point who was counting anymore? Loughery and the three who played in both games say their memory of that moment is getting trickier. So many years have passed, and as Loughery said: \u201cI got so many technicals in my career, they all seem the same.\u201d All that\u2019s left, really, is a box score that reads like it bypassed the auto-correct. Three players listed for both teams in the same game? And just for fun, guess who met in the first round of the playoffs that year? Nets vs. Sixers, won by Philly. No matter what happens in the NBA between now and Thursday\u2019s trade deadline, no matter the names or the number of players involved or how controversial the trades may be, those deals can only raise questions. They will never create a trivia question. * * * Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. "},{"id":41909,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/dallas-mavericks-legend-dirk-nowitzkis-name-misspelled-game-jersey","date":"2018-02-06","title":"Dallas Mavericks legend Dirk Nowitzki's name misspelled on game jersey","content":"Surefire Hall of Famer Dirk Nowitzki added to his immense list of accomplishments Monday, becoming the sixth player in NBA history to surpass 50,000 career minutes . Many teams are primed for a trade. Some need a shake-up, some need an upgrade, some need to unload salary, and almost every team would love to get their hands on more draft picks. But finding deals that work for both teams is easier said than done. And if you want a first round pick for a player you don\u2019t need, you may have to also take on some salary you don\u2019t want, like the Chicago Bulls did when they acquired Omer Asik in the deal that sent Nikola Mirotic to New Orleans. There are a lot of contracts out there that teams would love to unload and few teams with the flexibility to absorb those contracts. There are even fewer teams that can realistically have their eyes on winning a championship four months from now. The Golden State Warriors are too good. Some teams may want to add a piece to increase their chances of making the playoffs and becoming somewhat relevant (success in this league doesn\u2019t have to be defined by championships). Others may see an opportunity to supplant the Cleveland Cavaliers in the Eastern Conference. But there is probably only one team that can approach the deadline with this season being its No. 1 priority and with realistic thoughts about beating the Warriors in a playoff series. If you have an issue with the rankings, or have a question or comment for John Schuhmann, send him an e-mail or contact him via Twitter . * * *"},{"id":47465,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-san-antonio-spurs-point-guard-legacy-dejounte-murray-tony-parker","date":"2018-02-05","title":"Change at point guard shows 'Spurs Way' still undefeated in San Antonio","content":" Like being coach of the NFL\u2019s Pittsburgh Steelers, starting point guard for the San Antonio Spurs is not a job that comes open very often. Steelers coaches since 1969: Chuck Noll, Bill Cowher (1992), Mike Tomlin (2007). Spurs starting point guards (not counting guys who\u2019ve started there occasionally over the years because of injuries) since the 1994-95 season: Avery Johnson, Antonio Daniels\/Terry Porter (2000-01), Tony Parker (2001-02), Dejounte Murray (2017-18). Four point guards in almost a quarter century. As my friend Tony likes to say: that\u2019s it. That\u2019s the list .  That there was little fanfare in San Antonio when Murray took over for good on Jan. 21 , was \u2026 because San Antonio. The Spurs wouldn\u2019t make any public noise if the duplex in which they were all living burst into flames at 3 a.m. Hello, 911. There is a combustion event occurring at our domicile. Please do not send any assistance. We just thought it prudent and appropriate to inform you that we will be attaching a hose to the nearest available hydrant. No, we\u2019ll do it ourselves. Thank you for your time. But it doesn\u2019t mean it wasn\u2019t a big, big deal. And even a young guy like Murray understood the significance of it, and was willing to fall in line to do it \u201cThe Spurs Way .\u201d He knows that, one day, he\u2019ll be at the other end of this. \u201cAs you see, they do the right things,\u201d Murray said at the Spurs\u2019 practice facility last week. \u201cThey\u2019ve got the right people, the character, people that\u2019s willing to help you get better, from watching film, to strength, to shooting, to ballhandling. They have everything. Everybody\u2019s comfortable with each other; everyone respects one another. It\u2019s just a blessing to be around so (many) great people. There\u2019s not one person that I can tell you steered me the wrong way. Everybody is supportive of one another, we respect one another. The love is all one. It\u2019s a family here.\u201d Parker \u2014 even an older, diminished, oft-injured, 35-year-old Parker \u2014 is a seminal figure in Spurs history, a man and player who commands respect in that locker room because of what he\u2019s accomplished over 16-plus NBA seasons. A rookie starter at age 19. A six-time All-Star. A Finals MVP . A future first ballot Hall of Famer. And someone who killed himself when he absolutely did not have to in order to get back on the court this season . It took Spurs coach Gregg Popovich a couple of days to summon up the nerve to finally have the talk with one of his all-time favorite players, the tough love combined with allowing Parker to spend summer after summer playing for his beloved French national team over the years manifest of that feeling. It wasn\u2019t a long talk between coach and player. The message was simple: it\u2019s time. And, this is permanent. It would be a lie to say Parker was happy. He\u2019s a mighty competitor. But he accepted Popovich\u2019s decision, just as Tim Duncan and Manu Ginobili and others in recent years have as their roles were changed and reduced. \u201dA lot of people don\u2019t understand the relationship I have with Pop, the respect we have for each other,\u201d Parker said. \u201cAnd so, for me, it\u2019s like, I\u2019ll trust him. Whatever happens. I have a feeling that he\u2019ll take care of me my whole career, and I know he will until I\u2019m finished. When he told me he thought it was best for the team, me coming off the bench and play with Manu, maybe we\u2019ll bring some energy and stuff like that, I believe him. I trust him. And if he thinks we can do something even better with for team, I\u2019m fine with it. I\u2019ve been very blessed to be with the Spurs all those years, and that\u2019s part of our mentality. Me, Manu, Timmy, always sharing for the team.\u201d Often, \u2019culture\u2019 in an organization means \u2019we do what the best player wants us to do.\u2019 That\u2019s not culture; that\u2019s appropriation masquering as standards. The Spurs have true culture. It\u2019s a culture that could make David Robinson see he had to take a back seat almost immediately to Duncan , that saw Daniels realize the team would win more with Parker starting, that got Duncan to know when it was time for the offense to stop running through him and to start going through Kawhi Leonard . It is a culture that has seen Popovich reinvent the team over and over for 20 years. Most every player in the NBA wants to win. But most of them want to win without making any real sacrifices to their numbers \u2014 shots, money, whatever. (This is also true of many coaches, whose egos are just as big as most players. Trust me.) Parker is one of the winningest point guards of his generation \u2014 four championships, including that Finals MVP in 2007 as he led the Spurs to a four-game sweep of a young LeBron James and the Cleveland Cavaliers . But he is no longer that guy, even though he\u2019s getting paid like it ($15.4 million this season). \u201cYou have to look at the big picture,\u201d Parker said. \u201cFor me, as a professional, that\u2019s what you have to do that, anyway. You have to rehab. They pay me a lot of money, and so that\u2019s part of it. I never saw it like that. My relationship with Pop goes deeper than that. He knows how hard I worked to come back, and R.C. (Buford, the Spurs\u2019 general manager), too. So that\u2019s why, for me, I just look at the big picture. I really believe that they will take care of me.\u201d Murray does not lack for confidence. But he was still surprised to get the keys to the car so quickly. \u201cIt just shows how much of a pro he is, how much he\u2019s willing to sacrifice for the team,\u201d Murray said of Parker last week. \u201c\u2026I just try to motivate him to keep going. I always tell him, out of everything you did \u2014 winning championships, all the points you scored, anything \u2014 the thing that surprised me and shocked me the most and made me respect him even more was when he got hurt last year. To see him in here every day, coming in on crutches, trying to get to running and shooting, he was dedicated to get back. He said he was going to get back to play again, and that was the best thing that I\u2019ve seen from him.\u201d Murray looks like a colt during his two-year-old campaign \u2014 not ready for the Derby, but all legs and arms, potential pouring from his pores. His length makes him a more than capable defender already. The 3-point shot is not not his strength at present as he\u2019s made just 4 of 18 (22.2 percent) of them this season. That has to improve. But, he\u2019s not afraid of anybody. \u201c1,000%,\u201d DMs Jamal Crawford, the Wolves\u2019 guard and Murray\u2019s mentor; he\u2019s been shepherding Murray since the latter was a high school star in Crawford\u2019s native Seattle. Crawford has stayed on Murray since he came to the L, too, telling him \u201cEvery night, consistency. Not getting too high, or too low. It\u2019s a long journey, keep enjoying the process.\u201d Murray inhales rebounds at his position and became the third player in league history to grab 13 boards in a game in which he didn\u2019t score a point \u2013 which he did last week in a 106-104 win vs. Denver . The other players to accomplish the feat: Dennis Rodman and Petur Gudmundsson. (Petur Gudmundsson?) His 5.3 rebounds per game are sixth in the league among point guards , per NBA.com\/Stats, behind Russell Westbrook, Ben Simmons, Lonzo Ball, Kyle Lowry and Chris Paul. Murray started eight games out of injury necessity last season, but wasn\u2019t ready. He started the first seven this season while Parker was still completing the rehab from the quad injury that ended his season in Game 2 of the 2017 Western Conference semifinals . But after Murray struggled with consistency and his shot in October and early November, Popovich started Patty Mills 11 games to try and get some offensive punch on the floor. Coming off the bench in November, Murray\u2019s offensive rating rose signficantly. And when Parker got back on the court, while the team was still managing his starter minutes, they placed Murray down the stretch in several games. But that was off-Broadway stuff. The Spurs always had plans for the day their 2016 first-rounder would be the guy. They decided not to wait any longer once they were able to see Parker play a month\u2019s worth of games. \u201cInternally, this probably hasn\u2019t been any different than it appears externally,\u201d Buford said. \u201dJust as Timmy allowed Pop to coach him, and Manu allowed us to present our team in the best way possible, Tony, the day the discussion took place with Pop, those were his sentiments \u2026 we couldn\u2019t have done this without the professionalism Tony displayed \u2014 and the mentorship he continues to show to Dejounte.\u201d At least Murray\u2019s had half a season to get ready. Parker became the permanent starter in 2001 in his fifth professional game. He started all but one game the rest of that year, including all 10 playoff games. \u201cI think the biggest thing is staying positive, because you\u2019re going to have your ups and downs,\u201d Parker said. \u201cNow, a lot of people are going to have a lot of expectations of him, and you know how hard Pop is. You know how he is. At the point guard position, it\u2019s not easy to play for him \u2026 \u201cI\u2019m just trying to be there for (Murray), you know \u2014 support, whatever questions he has, and using all my knowledge that I have. I think I know the system even better than Pop. I\u2019m just trying to help him as much as I can. It\u2019s not going to be an easy transition, because as we play better teams, and playoffs, you know how hard it is.\u201d This regular season hasn\u2019t exactly been a cakewalk for the Spurs. Their 34-21 record belies the incredible job they\u2019ve done patching together lineups and wins. With Kawhi Leonard having played just nine games as recovering from his own quad injury has proven much tougher than expected, and Rudy Gay (heel) out since just after Christmas , San Antonio has had to make do with almost no proven scoring on the floor around All-Star LaMarcus Aldridge for long stretches. Murray is just one of several young players \u2014 as well as Brandon Paul, Davis Bertans, Bryn Forbes and Derrick White \u2014 who\u2019ve all played meaningful minutes already this year. \u201cWe\u2019ve had to invent ourselves almost every night with a different lineup,\u201d Buford said. \u201cPop\u2019s not going to let us feel sorry for ourselves. And it creates great opportunities. Do Bryn Forbes, does Davis get these chances in other places, if the circumstances had been as linear? I don\u2019t know.\u201d The Spurs, though, despite being outstanding again defensively (first in the league in points allowed ; second in Defensive Rating ; seventh in opponent field goal percentage and opponent 3-point percentage ), aren\u2019t clicking on all cylinders. San Antonio has plummeted offensively, falling from first in 3-point percentage last season to 17th this season . Their 9-7 January was their worst month since December, 2014, when they went 8-10. They have missed Leonard terribly, and an ESPN story last month saying that the relationship between the team and its newest franchise player had cooled over disagreements about treatment plans and other issues was noteworthy not because of its content, but because someone was willing to publicly cite disagreement with how the franchise was operating. When the franchise makes mistakes, it\u2019s usually the one that acknowledges them, as Popovich did when he said that he was wrong to try and change Aldridge\u2019s offensive game his first season in San Antonio.  Buford said last week that the issue of Leonard\u2019s rehab is \u201cnot chilling; it\u2019s challenging. But no one\u2019s put more time into than our group, and Kawhi, and we\u2019ve put all the resources we can, internally and externally, to get him feeling confident enough to play.\u201d So, while they wait for Leonard, they must win games with their young guys taking on bigger roles \u2014 and with Parker coaching Murray on the intricacies of the position \u2014 the hardest, by far, in basketball. \u201cHe\u2019s a great kid, and he listens,\u201d Parker said. \u201cHe wants to get better. And he understands what it takes to get better. He\u2019s in the gym. He works hard. For me, that\u2019s good to see that he wants to be better.\u201d It means Murray is getting a real-time education in Popology \u2014 the screaming, followed by the arm around the shoulder, followed by the well-timed moment of inquiry and curiosity at practice, or a video session. \u201cPop don\u2019t play that,\u201d Murray said. \u201cIt\u2019s not BS. It\u2019s all stuff that makes sense. That\u2019s the big picture about it \u2014 everything he\u2019s talking about, everything he\u2019s yelling about, it all means something. It\u2019s the right thing.\u201d And it means Murray is around when Duncan and Matt Bonner returned to the gym last week so the Spurs could have enough bodies to run four on four in practice. \u201cIt just shows me their love for the game,\u201d Murray said. \u201cThey\u2019re retired. They could be running businesses and never coming to see a court again. They put so much work in. But just to see how much they love the game, and I look at it the same way. I really love the game. \u201cI don\u2019t do this because I like it or it\u2019s fun, or the stuff that comes with it. I love this. I want to be great. To see Tim come in here \u2014 he comes in here to lift; he comes in here to shoot; he comes in here to play. It\u2019s crazy. Seeing that stuff, it just shows that after basketball, it\u2019s going to be the same thing (for me). I want to be in the gym. You can\u2019t go away. It don\u2019t matter how much money you make, or how much money you got, if you love the game, you always find your way (to) playing the game.\u201d Speaking of which, Parker is still playing. In his first eight games in his new role, he\u2019s averaging 8.6 points in 18.9 minutes and shooting 45.6 percent. He badly wants to play 20 seasons in San Antonio before calling it a career. That would mean three more seasons after this one. He trusts the Spurs will get him there. So, he looks at the positives \u2014 a last chance to play next to Ginobili (\u201cI haven\u2019t played with Manu in five years,\u201d Parker said), and a chance to pay it forward. \u201cIt\u2019s not easy,\u201d he said. \u201cYou still have to digest and stuff like that. But, for me, I knew it was not going to last forever. I understand that the NBA is a young league. And I was the oldest starter (at point guard) the last three years. So I understand at some point, it\u2019s even better for my career to come off the bench. If, hopefully, Pop gives me my last three years, I can have great energy those last three years coming off the bench.\u201d There have been more fits and starts this season, maybe, than normal. The Spurs aren\u2019t championship material. But they\u2019re still 34-21, still capable of a push if Leonard comes back healthy at some point this season \u2014 in a perfect world, maybe after the All-Star break. They did throttle the Rockets in the 2017 playoffs and they were up 20 on the Warriors in Game 1 of the 2017 Western Conference finals when Leonard landed on Zaza Pachulia\u2019s foot . They have institutional memory, and they have grit, and now they have a new point guard to run the show until 2030 or so. \u201cWe\u2019re working,\u201d Murray said. \u201cWe\u2019re working every day. That\u2019s like, ooh, Tim Duncan to Manu to TP? All these banners, they did that. It\u2019s crazy. But at the same time, it\u2019s the next man up. And that\u2019s how I approach things: if I\u2019m the next man up, I\u2019ve got to be ready to go. Like I said, I\u2019m working every day, and I\u2019m showing them that I\u2019m here to learn and get better every day. That\u2019s all I can say. \u201cAs long as I stay healthy and (with) God\u2019s will, I\u2019m going to work my ass off. I\u2019m going to continue to study the game and learn and learn and learn. \u2019Cause as the point guard, you\u2019re the quarterback, you\u2019ve got to know everything. I want that. Some dudes shy away from it and are scared of this type of position. I\u2019m built for it. I fear nothing. I\u2019m going to continue to reach for everything I can.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. The Detroit Pistons and LA Clippers, for different reasons, went out on a ledge last Monday, then jumped , taking the proverbial leap of faith that what they\u2019d just done would ultimately work out. But both, stuck in NBA limbo, are gambling \u2014 gambling the jobs of those who worked out the deal, gambling the futures of the players that remain on their respective teams after. The only person who was sitting pretty after it was all done was the centerpiece of the deal. * Aldridge:  Clippers deal Griffin after superstar unable to elevate team Blake Griffin will get the $140 million coming to him through 2022 regardless. Now it\u2019s just coming out of Pistons owner Tom Gores\u2019 pocket, rather than Steve Ballmer\u2019s. (And, Griffin will have to have some clothes sent to Detroit. Other than that \u2026 he\u2019s good.) But for the Pistons, who got Griffin, Willie Reed, Brice Johnson and a second-round pick last Monday from the Clippers for Tobias Harris, Avery Bradley, Boban Marjanovic and a 2018 first-round pick, the decision to bring in the extremely skilled yet oft-injured five-time All-Star, and to pick up his price tag, wasn\u2019t a hard one at all. \u201cHow many chances do you get to acquire elite level talent?,\u201d Pistons Coach and President of Basketball Operations Stan Van Gundy said Sunday via text. \u201cAnd how often would we be able to get that talent if there wasn\u2019t some risk involved?\u201d Griffin has been laid low by significant injury in each of the last three regular seasons , and couldn\u2019t finish either of the Clippers\u2019 last two postseasons. Injuries have also cost him All-Star and Olympic team appearances the last few years. In the NBA, there tend to be two types of guys when it comes to injuries: those that return from them in a few days, and those that return in a few weeks. Griffin has been the latter. It\u2019s not his fault, any more than it\u2019s someone\u2019s fault when they develop an allergy to something. You don\u2019t ask to break out in hives when you eat peanuts; Griffin isn\u2019t looking to get hurt. It just happens. But where were the Pistons going? They had lost eight straight just before they made the deal, falling out of the playoff race in the Eastern Conference. Their great start the first fortnight of the season had been long forgotten. Reggie Jackson, their starting point guard, was hurt again and out for a long time with an ankle sprain . Van Gundy reached the decision a couple of years ago that it would be difficult, if not impossible, to get free agents to sign with Detroit. So he brought Jackson in from Oklahoma City , Harris from Orlando and Marcus Morris from Phoenix . Griffin was a logical extension of that philosophy. * Trade Tracker:  Every deal in NBA this season If he\u2019d been a free agent in 2017 and it would have cost the Pistons Harris and Bradley to sign him, no one would have thought twice about it. He\u2019s a superstar, and the Pistons, as much as they try to promote All-Star Andre Drummond, don\u2019t have one. \u201cWe\u2019re serious about winning and we\u2019re not afraid to make a bold move and seize the opportunity,\u201d Gores said via e-mail Sunday night. \u201cChances like this don\u2019t come along very often. The move is not without risk and we gave up a lot to get him. We know that basketball is a game full of risk, but it was really clear that he is worth it.\u201d (They insist that they got Griffin for his talent alone, but the Pistons haven\u2019t drawn well at all in their first year in their new building, Little Caesars Arena, which they share with the NHL\u2019s Red Wings. Getting a high-flying, crowd-pleasing player like Griffin can\u2019t possibly hurt with ticket and sponsorship sales. Detroit drew announced crowds of 17,841 in Griffin\u2019s debut Thursday against the Memphis Grizzlies and 18,747 Saturday against the Miami Heat .) People throughout the league had indicated just after the New Year that the Clippers were making Griffin available in potential trade scenarios. But this specific deal didn\u2019t come to fruition until near the end of the month. And Gores didn\u2019t hesitate when the proposed deal was put on his table. \u201cDon\u2019t really want to get into internal discussions,\u201d Van Gundy said, \u201cbut it didn\u2019t take much selling. Tom was all for it.\u201d Said Gores: \u201cI was involved from the beginning of the discussions. Once we realized this was a real deal, that it was a real opportunity, I told our guys we should get it done. Keep our young guys, keep some flexibility, but get it done.\u201d The Clippers had sold Griffin that he\u2019d get his jersey retired in Staples Center someday, and that he\u2019d be a Clipper for life, when they got him to sign a five-year, $173 million deal in July . Yes, Chris Paul was gone, but the team was going to build again around Griffin, just as it had after making him the first pick of the 2009 Draft . That notion lasted about as long as Griffin did before spraining the MCL in his left knee just after Thanksgiving, costing him another month on the shelf. That injury, coming so soon into the season, \u201cscared the (bleep) out of Steve when it happened,\u201d one league source said. Suddenly, Ballmer\u2019s team wasn\u2019t as tied to Griffin as it purported to be in the summer. Those ties were coming loose even before Griffin\u2019s injury. Ballmer had hired Hall of Famer Jerry West from the Golden State Warriors in June as a consultant , and at a not-inconsiderable cost. You don\u2019t bring \u201cThe Logo\u201d in to say \u2018everything looks great here; carry on.\u2019 And, changes were made quickly. Doc Rivers lost his title as president of basketball operations last August, with Lawrence Frank taking over as the team\u2019s chief decision maker, and L.A. hiring highly regarded Michael Winger from Oklahoma City to further bolster the front office as general manager. The Clippers were heading toward a teardown anyway, and especially after suffering a rasher of injuries early in the season. They lost guard Patrick Beverley, who was supposed to take over for Paul at the point, for the season to a knee injury in November , and key acqusitions like forward Danilo Gallinari and Milos Teodosic have each missed weeks of action with various injuries. It left Los Angeles on the periphery of the playoff picture in the Western Conference, depending on G-League talents and players on two-way contracts to keep the Clippers afloat. So \u2026 why are we on the hook to Blake for 34 large for five years, again? By getting Griffin off the books, at least the Clips would be able to take a real shot at the likes of LeBron James, Kyrie Irving, Kevin Love and others who could be available in the summer of 2019. And that\u2019s the world where Jerry West, like Bumpy Johnson in the Cotton Club, likes to dance. \u201cLet\u2019s say the Clippers team would have made the playoffs,\u201d West told host Suzy Shuster on the \u201cNot Just Sports\u201d podcast last week . \u201cThere\u2019s no guarantee, because we\u2019ve had so many injuries, period. There was no guarantee, even with Blake here, that we were going to make the playoffs. But I think the one thing that I would say to you and to everyone is, would you rather this Clipper team, the way it was presently constructed, let\u2019s say play the Warriors (in the first round) and lose every game by 20, 25 points. That\u2019s what was going to be. I don\u2019t think anyone who\u2019s competitive, and cares about winning, wants that. No one. And certainly not, our fans don\u2019t.\u201d With Harris, just 25 and on a very good by today\u2019s standards deal, along with a healthy Gallinari, Teodosic and Austin Rivers, the Clippers expect to at least be competitive for the next year, whether or not they keep or re-sign DeAndre Jordan and Lou Williams, or trade them by Thursday\u2019s deadline. At least they hope so: \u201cfree agents don\u2019t want to join a (bleep) team,\u201d another source said. And, they expect the L.A. market to work for them in 2019. It worked for Griffin, on and off the court. But not well enough to keep him in town. The Pistons don\u2019t care. All they know is their game with the Clippers this Friday is suddenly on national television ( 7 ET, ESPN ). \u201cMore than a great basketball player, he\u2019s a natural leader,\u201d Gores e-mailed Sunday. \u201cI spent several hours with him after we finished the deal. He has great character, believes in hard work and competing to the highest standard. He\u2019s such a competitor, he wants to win, and you can see it in the way he plays.\u201d If Detroit was ebullient, the mood among Clippers folks as they heard the news last Monday was \u201cterrible,\u201d one source said. A team that had competed on the highest levels in the West for the last four seasons was suddenly back in the middle of the pack, betting on hope rather than what they actually had in the building. It was discomfiting. But it was reality. \u201cIn this business,\u201d West told Shuster, \u201cthe biggest risk takers do the best. Not the biggest gamblers. I don\u2019t think we took a gamble. Period. And I think we\u2019re gonna do just fine with what we have.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":47455,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-qa-new-orleans-pelicans-emeka-okafor","date":"2018-02-05","title":"Morning Tip Q&A: Emeka Okafor","content":"They will never need to hold a telethon for Emeka Okafor. Dude graduated with a finance degree in three years with honors and a 3.8 overall GPA at Connecticut, where he led the Huskies to the national championship in 2004. (I know this acutely because during the evening the Huskies beat Duke in the national semifinals at the Final Four, my wife turned to me at some point in the second half and said \u2018we have to go to the hospital. It\u2019s time.\u2019 It took some time \u2014 maybe he wanted to see the end of the game \u2014 but our first born was delivered the morning of the national championship game between UConn and Georgia Tech.) All of this to say, Emeka Okafor is a smart guy who would get along just fine without basketball. Except, he can\u2019t. Which may explain why he\u2019s in New Orleans this morning with the Pelicans, on a 10-day contract signed Saturday , at 35, more than four years removed from the last time he played in the NBA, with the Wizards in the 2012-13 season. He played well in Washington, but a herniated disc in his neck, which was diagnosed before the Wizards sent him to Phoenix in October, 2013, for Marcin Gortat , kept Okafor \u2014 the second pick overall in the 2004 Draft, by Charlotte, after Orlando took Dwight Howard first \u2014 kept him from ever playing for the Suns, or anyone else. Rehabbing to get back on the court, the former Rookie of the Year winner Okafor paused to first get married (many of us who cover the league know his wife, Ilana, who did PR for the NBA for many years) and then have two beautiful kids. But his thoughts were always toward a return to playing. The problem was, out of sight, out of mind. As 2014 turned into 2015 and 2016, teams moved on, though the Cleveland Cavaliers, Golden State Warriors and Miami Heat all talked with him over the years . Finally, in 2017, Okafor was cleared to play, and signed a camp deal with the 76ers last fall . He didn\u2019t make it out of preseason, but he opted to continue his comeback with Philly\u2019s G League team in Delaware , the 87ers ( \u201cthe first time I heard he was gonna be my roommate, I was really shocked,\u201d said his Sevens teammate, Julian Jacobs, \u201c\u2018 cause when he got drafted, I was in, like, fourth grade \u201d).  Okafor played in 26 games for Delaware, averaging 6.8 points and 4.7 rebounds . The Pelicans came calling after losing DeMarcus Cousins for the season with an Achilles\u2019 tear a week ago. Nothing is guaranteed other than the next 10 days, which is a new place to be for a guy who\u2019s been a starter and made man almost all of his basketball career. But Okafor will take the leap. He\u2019s strong enough again now to handle the landing. Me: You have a beautiful wife whom we all love, and two beautiful kids, and I know you probably have the first dime you made playing pro ball. So, why? Why are you doing this? Emeka Okafor: You know, when I first got injured, the goal was to always come back. It never dawned on me that, hey, man, I\u2019m done, let me quit. When I\u2019m embarking on the rehab process, I didn\u2019t plan on it taking four years. I didn\u2019t plan on taking four years off. The whole time my mind was like, I gotta go back, I gotta go back, I gotta go back. I never lost that hunger to play and I still felt I could play. When it came finally time to actually get on the court, I tried out for some teams. Understandably so, they were like, hey, man, you\u2019ve been gone for four years. There were questions on whether I could do it, why I was doing it. Are you going to quit in the middle of it? So the G League was the option as a proving ground to demonstrate the want to and the capability to play. I was like, okay, I get it. If this is what the process dictates, I want to get back. And if this is the way back in, this is what I have to do. Long story short, the goal was always to come back and play. I wanted to play. I felt I could play. As an athlete, I think we all have this drive that just keeps us going. For me, I wasn\u2019t going to stop unless the situation forced me to stop. I just couldn\u2019t lay down and go \u2018okay, I\u2019m done.\u2019 I think I can go unless I\u2019m told otherwise, or forced otherwise. Me: You were pretty decent in camp for the Sixers. How disappointing was it you couldn\u2019t stick there, after all the work you\u2019d put in just to get back on the court? EO: This has been a learning process for me. To be fair, I thought that me getting back in would a little be easier than it was. I was like, okay, with my experience and what I\u2019ve done, I thought I would be at a minimum contract, that a team would take a chance. I didn\u2019t really, until I put myself in the eyes of a GM, in terms of being gone, and I saw all the work, I understood the business aspects and the risks associated, and the concerns associated with me getting back in. In understanding that, I understood the time frame of when my window of opportunity would actually be. Once I understood rosters and contracts and guaranteed dates, and when 10 days were able to start, and trade deadlines, there\u2019s just all these other aspects of the NBA that I didn\u2019t know about, because it didn\u2019t pertain to me during my career. Now, at this point, when I was trying to get back in and I saw all these workings, I was like, okay, this is a completely different animal than what I thought \u2026 especially since call-ups don\u2019t start until Jan. 5th or 10th, or whatever it was, and I understand from the point of a GM, hey, let\u2019s see if this guy even wants to do it, or if he can do it. I understood that no one\u2019s going to jump out immediately. I understood the lay of the land. I didn\u2019t really take it personally. Me: Besides the obvious opportunity, what was appealing about New Orleans to you? EO: I\u2019ve been here. The city\u2019s familiar, the GM\u2019s familiar, the staff\u2019s familiar, the arena\u2019s familiar. To be honest, my thought process was, just for the sake of being comfortable, if I could somehow land in a place where I\u2019ve already been, that would be pretty cool. The fact that that worked out is awesome. Me: You know the game and how the game has evolved since you\u2019ve been out, with every big being a stretch four or five now. How have you incorporated some of that into your game? EO: I\u2019ve been working on threes and dribbling skills. I haven\u2019t implemented the three yet. I\u2019m capable of doing it. But, to be fair, there\u2019s certain classics that never go out of style. Being able to screen and roll hard, play solid defense and rebound the basketball and finish around the rim, there\u2019s always gong to be a need for that, no matter what happens. I feel like that\u2019s my core value, to be able to do that extremely well. That\u2019s going to be able to fit somewhere and somehow, in whatever era you\u2019re going to be in. Especially in the pick and roll dominated game, you have to be able to set screens very well, and you have to roll. The ability to set screens, roll hard and finish at the rim, whether it be dunking or being able to finish with finesse, is what\u2019s going to open your shooters and your stretch fours and whatever else you\u2019re trying to set up. That aspect is still important. And being able to defend and rebound the basketball. Those have been my core values and I\u2019ve made sure that I\u2019m solid in that foundation. But I understand the way the game\u2019s gone. I\u2019m going to work on these other aspects and be able to use them when necessary. It\u2019s in my back pocket. If it\u2019s needed, I can pull it out and use it. Me: How do you plan when you\u2019re on a 10-day contract? EO: Literally, you can\u2019t. I was in Grand Rapids getting ready to play the Drive, and I got a call: hey, man, we need you on a plane in seven hours. Granted, the road trip I was on was only for one day, so I packed two or three pairs of socks, two pairs of underwear, a shirt or two, and that was it. That\u2019s what I have with me right now. And it\u2019s like, okay, now I\u2019m in New Orleans, and it\u2019s like, figure it out. New offensive scheme, new teammates, new whatever. Granted, it\u2019s the same city but it\u2019s like, bam. And coming from a place of guaranteed contracts, guaranteed playing time, I knew where I fit in. There was no volatility. To just, straight up, volatility, seat of your pants, figure it out as you go. No real compassion. Well, it\u2019s compassion, but it\u2019s like, the window of what you can do is like, all right, produce now. Right now. It\u2019s interesting. It\u2019s a different side. It\u2019s an adventure for me. I\u2019m enjoying it. It\u2019s giving me a well-rounded basketball experience and given me perspective and even more appreciation for the grind. But iit is different. You can\u2019t plan for it. JYour mindset, to be honest, is just stay ready. My whole thing is, be prepared for your opportunity, so when your opportunity presents itself, in whatever fashion, you\u2019re ready. Because you\u2019re not going to know what\u2019s going to happen . You have to be prepared to be prepared. Me: Did you have to have any conversations with your family about this, or did they just say, go? EO: Ilana, she\u2019s awesome. I\u2019m very fortunate to have her as my wife. Great mother. She wanted me to be back damn near more than I wanted to be back. Her dad was a ref (former Supervisor of Officials Ronnie Nunn), and she worked in the NBA. She knows the business. She knows what it is. There was no conversation necessary, \u2018cause she already knew the deal. It was like, all right, this is what we\u2019ve got to do. She was with me the whole rehab process. She understood the drive and what I was going for and why I was doing it. She understands how special a thing it is to be part of the NBA. If there\u2019s a chance, you want to be part of it as long as you can. It\u2019s a very special experience. It\u2019s everybody\u2019s dream to be there. If you have access to it, you just don\u2019t stop. You don\u2019t just lay down. You go until you can\u2019t. Me: You ever played for or on the same staff with Alvin? I know you weren\u2019t playing in Phoenix, but I didn\u2019t know if your time there overlapped. EO: No. Me: So what has he said to you about expectations and what he wants from you? EO: Ah, nothing. Literally, I haven\u2019t talked to him. There hasn\u2019t even been time. It might have been the 24-hour mark today. Right now, and even that\u2019s marked by plane time and travel and sleep. Right now, I\u2019m at the practice facility, getting ready to go over the plays. Me: I think a lot of guys want to go out on their own terms. I wonder how important that was to you: that no matter what happens, just getting back on an NBA court and being able to play allows you to say, at least, I made it back, and if it\u2019s this year or next year or whenever, if I leave, it won\u2019t be because of injury; it\u2019ll just be because it\u2019s time. EO: My thing is, go until you can\u2019t. Go until it just doesn\u2019t make sense anymore. I don\u2019t feel like that time has come. My injury happened, and I was like, no, I still can go. Work back and give it your all, and if it doesn\u2019t work out, hey, you tried. What are you going to do? It\u2019s sports; stuff happens. But until that moment, you just go until the horn stops. Me: What do you think you can provide to a team \u2014 and I don\u2019t mean the Xs and Os, skill sets part. I mean as a guy, as a person, as a teammate. EO: Just wealth of knowledge and experience. In terms of professionalism, how to be a basketball professional. I think I\u2019m very good at that. My thing is, I\u2019m always trying to, people have helped me out and taught me. I\u2019m trying to help and teach young guys. Whether it\u2019s basketball, life, or just any aspect. Letting them know, make sure you enjoy what\u2019s going on. Or giving them certain aspects of their game. Making sure that people grow, whether it\u2019s on the court or off the court, making sure they\u2019re aware of certain pitfalls that may or may not happen. You see the road. And younger guys, they don\u2019t see the big picture, see the road. They might get caught up in some obstacles that may not be as big as they perceive, or get caught up in stuff that\u2019s really, really not important. So just making sure that they know, this is what you need to worry about. This thing here is minor. This is the big picture. Me: Who were some of the guys that really helped you? EO: My rookie season, I had Brevin Knight. He was a good vet. And I had Steve Smith, also, my first year, he was a good vet. And the rest were actually very, very young teams. Bernie Bickerstaff, he was an awesome first coach. A lot of these lessons, you don\u2019t even realize at that time. You realize it later: oh, man, that\u2019s what they were talking about. Little conversations that happened between players on the court. In the offseason. I was in L.A. a lot and would play pickup. Just certain conversations you\u2019d hear, people just talking. At various points in your life you\u2019d reflect back and be like, okay, that\u2019s what they were talking about. Then we give it an overall grade on a scale of 1-5 Robert Horrys, named for the patron saint of last-second answered prayers. * * * All-Star forward Al Horford has done it all this season for the surging Celtics, who many thought would struggle after newly acquired Gordon Hayward went down with a season-ending injury in the first game. DIFFICULTY: Horford\u2019s 2-pointer wasn\u2019t a long one (15 feet), but there was nothing easy about it. With four seconds left, Jaylen Brown gets the inbound pass on the right wing to Horford, who had sprinted across the lane to accept it. Draped by a tough defender, the Blazers\u2019 Al Farouq Aminu, Horford dribbles twice, leans his back into Aminu slightly, then steps away from the direction of the basket and elevates for the fadeaway. The turnaround shot bangs the lower back of the rim and flushes for the game-winner. GAME SITUATION: Sitting atop the Eastern Conference standings, the Celtics had battled back from a slow start against the Trial Blazers, a Western Conference team likely headed for the playoffs. But trailing by one point and with the possession, it was do-or-die for the Celtics. Boston\u2019s confidence in calling Horford\u2019s number \u2014 and the confidence he had shown in leading his team this day \u2014 was rewarded. Failure to finish after the comeback would have been deflating, even if this wasn\u2019t a conference matchup. CELEBRATION: It wasn\u2019t the seventh game of a playoff series and not much was at stake in this game, other than momentum. But Horford\u2019s fist pump and mobbing by his teammates as the crowd at TD Garden roared was testimony to Horford\u2019s standing with his teammates and fans. Again. Three hundred and fifty-nine days had come and gone since Parker had participated in an NBA game for the Milwaukee Bucks, a stretch even longer than the first time he ruptured and rehabbed the same left anterior cruciate ligament in his same left knee. That one cost him 317 days, from the middle of December in his rookie season to the start of his second in 2015-16. Which means Parker has been hurt and hopeful more than he\u2019s been happy or helpful to a team that drafted him No. 2 overall in 2014 to play opposite Giannis Antetokounmpo on the Milwaukee front line. That\u2019s where they were against the New York Knicks at the BMO Harris Bradley Center, for much of Parker\u2019s 14 minutes and 48 seconds (the Bucks\u2019 medical staff had him limited to 15, max). It\u2019s a tandem that is going to have to click if Milwaukee hopes to reach its goals of a top-four finish in the Eastern Conference and, in the playoffs, advancing to the conference semifinals. If only the teamwork and chemistry would stop getting so significantly interrupted. Parker looked eager, bouncy and only a time or two tentative against the Knicks, subbing in for the first time at 5:25 of the opening quarter. The crowd of 18,717 welcomed him onto the court with their applause and Parker acknowledged them by scoring seven points. He hit his first shot, a jumper from the right baseline, then appeard to lack a little lift when he tried a reverse slam. When he broke out and took a long outlet pass from Khris Middleton, Parker had to slam on the brakes before finishing with a layup. The knee held. The crowd exhaled. \u201cI just wanted to stay composed,\u201d Parker said later, reviewing his night. \u201cNot trying to get carried away. Trying to stay right. Pretty much distracted, cuz I heard the crowd. I had to calm down and kind of teach myself like, you\u2019ve still got to play. You\u2019ve still got to show the people out there [what] you were working so hard [for].\u201d Parker\u2019s minutes leash dictated that he wasn\u2019t around when the game was decided. Tied at 90-90 from the 2:00 minute mark of the fourth, Antetokounmpo eventually put Milwaukee up with 1.9 seconds left with a spinning, long-armed, left-handed layup past Enes Kanter. Then the Bucks got Tim Hardaway Jr., who\u2019d shot 2 for 10 to that point, to miss one more from 30 feet. Parker watched the drama at the end of an otherwise grinding game from the side. But in time, assuming the knee repair holds, he will be on the floor for such moments, even in the middle of them. \u201cOnly way to go here is goin\u2019 up,\u201d he said after finishing with 12 points on 4-of-7 shooting. \u201cCan\u2019t be below any point that I\u2019ve been at any stages of these few months. Anything I do from this point on is to go up, and it\u2019s a blessing. \u2026 Any opportunity I get, it beats being on the couch for three months at a time.\u201d Parker has missed 144 of a possible 297 games with his injuries, either of which could have spelled ruin \u2013 or still could \u2013 for his budding career. He was playing at a near-All-Star level (20.1 ppg, 6.2 rpg, 36.5 3-point pg) when he got hurt, without contact, against Miami last Feb. 8. Facing another year of monotonous and grueling rehab work, far away from cheers, bright lights or competition with anyone but himself, Parker doubled down on the things that got him through his first comeback. Writing about it in a piece posted by The Players Tribune on Friday, Parker explained that his faith grew deeper, his anger buoyed him through much of his morning-and-evenings regimen, and the late-night drives in his \u201964 Cadillac Coupe De Ville kept him centered through the ordeal. \u201cIt went quick, because I got lost into the process I was having,\u201d Parker told NBA.com Friday. It did not go quick for the Bucks. In Parker\u2019s absence, they crammed in their second playoff appearance without him (he missed their first-round battles with Chicago in 2015 and Toronto last spring). They replaced the only coach he\u2019d known as a pro, Jason Kidd, with interim Joe Prunty. Antetokounmpo blew up as one of the NBA\u2019s hottest new stars. Eric Bledsoe arrived in trade as the team\u2019s new point guard. Meanwhile, Parker failed to negotiate a contract extension to stay with the Bucks. That gives him only a few months to fit back into the team\u2019s rotation, make a difference and demonstrate his value heading toward restricted free agency in July. To this point, the Bucks are 68-85 in the regular-season games Parker has played for them, 76-68 in the ones he has not. But there have been too many other moving pieces in Milwaukee in that time to rate him on record alone. What matters for Antetokounmpo, Prunty and the rest is how well the 6-foot-8, 246-pound Parker blends in and complements what the others are doing. As a wingman to the Greek Freak, Parker has a game better suited to pounding down low, while offering another shooter with decent range for Antetokounmpo\u2019s kick-outs. Defensively, he matched up against some of the bigger Knicks, including Kristaps Porzingis, Kanter and Michael Beasley. The Bucks were without guard Malcolm Brogdon, the 2017 Rookie of the Year who will miss six to eight weeks after suffering a partially torn left quadriceps tendon Thursday at Minnesota. Brogdon\u2019s exit just as Parker returns is cruelly reminiscent of last season, when Middleton came back from hamstring surgery in Game 51, the same night Parker went down. Handing off an injury baton is no way to prep for postseason success, but the Bucks feel they have sufficient time to set themselves up. \u201cThe potential is big,\u201d Antetokounmpo said. \u201cHe can be a top 15, top 20 player in this league, so just having a guy like that next to you [helps]. He\u2019s a big threat. Just having Jabari out there, the defense is going to pay attention to him. So when they pay attention to him, they pay less attention to me, to Khris, to Bledsoe.\u201d Said Middleton, \u201cI\u2019m proud of him. That\u2019s tough. To go through what he went through twice and come back and play like nothing\u2019s ever happened, that\u2019s great. I know there a lot going through his mind, but he did a great job of just going out there and playing basketball. \u201cIt is special. He\u2019s one of our best players. He\u2019s one of the key pieces who can take us farther than we\u2019ve been. So having that means putting smiles on everybody\u2019s faces.\u201d Whatever anxiety the Bucks have about the fragility of the tissue and grafts in Parker\u2019s repaired knee, that is balanced for the moment by their teammate\u2019s demeanor and perspective. Antetokounmpo said late Friday that they all have learned \u201cpatience\u201d from Parker\u2019s challenge, even as he\u2019s made peace with what\u2019s been and what shall be. As he noted in the Players\u2019 Tribune piece: \u201cWhat matters to me is not accolades or awards or headlines. That\u2019s just not where I\u2019m at. After injuries and rehabs, what matters to me is what I can control. What matters to me is going after what I love and then working as hard as I can to protect it.\u201d It matters to Milwaukee, that\u2019s for sure. The place: the gymnasium in Vienna, Austria, where Rainer \u00d6mer and Martina Poeltl practiced and honed the skills that earned them roster spots on Austria\u2019s national volleyball men\u2019s and women\u2019s teams.  The child: Jakob Poeltl, dragged along, running loose, playing around and messing with sports equipment from whomever, wherever. Legend has it the energized 6-year-old one day picked up a bigger, heavier, pebble-grained orange ball he\u2019d found and, in that instant, began straying from his parents\u2019 sport. The result: Poeltl is a promising, second-year big man for the Toronto Raptors, the first Austrian to reach the NBA and a fellow for whom dunks have replaced spikes entirely. \u201cI was more in basketball,\u201d Poeltl said before a recent game in Chicago. The 22-year-old, now 7 feet and 248 pounds, pronounces his name \u201cYA-kub PURR-tuhl.\u201d \u201cI did play volleyball with my parents when we went on holidays. But it was never anything serious, it was always just fun. They taught me a lot \u2014 I think I\u2019m half-decent at volleyball. Obviously I couldn\u2019t play it at a very high level like they did, but I still know some stuff from back in the day that they showed me.\u201d  Still knows some stuff? That\u2019s intriguing as Poeltl continues to develop as an active, mobile center who backs up Jonas Valanciunas. Is it possible that any aspects of the family business transfer to NBA play, offensively or defensively? Besides the high fives, that is. \u201cA big chunk,\u201d Poeltl said. \u201cI got my height from them. I got my athletic ability probably, to a certain extent, from them too. Always, growing up, I was around sports. It was a very active family, I guess. I was always moving. They say I couldn\u2019t stop running around.\u201d That\u2019s a good start for a big man in today\u2019s NBA. There\u2019s more.  \u201cHis footwork is unbelievable,\u201d Raptors coach Dwane Casey said. \u201cThe athletic genes are there. Quick feet, great hands, good hand-eye coordination. And he picks up stuff so fast. I think it comes from being around that athletic background.\u201d Said teammate C.J. Miles, new to Toronto this season with an inside glimpse at Poeltl\u2019s development: \u201cHe\u2019s extremely mobile for his size. Great hands. His athleticism shows up on both ends, defensively and offensively. He\u2019s got a tremendous feel for the NBA. \u201cHis agility. His feet. He\u2019s got good bounce off the floor.\u201d Volleyball and the NBA have a pretty long history. Wilt Chamberlain, after wrapping his legendary hoops career, picked up the sport and played it well into his 40s. He played both beach and indoor versions and was quoted in his 1991 book, \u201cA View From Above,\u201d saying, \u201cFor a long time, volleyball became as big a part of my life as basketball once was.\u201d He even got involved in the mid-1970s with, and lent legitimacy to, the short-lived pro International Volleyball Association. Bill Walton, not surprising given his southern California roots and nature-loving way, played beach volleyball. So does his son, current Lakers coach Luke Walton. On a recent trip to Chicago, the younger Walton talked about how forgiving the sand is for an NBA player whose legs and bodies don\u2019t need any extra pounding. The Waltons honed their games with the help of Greg Lee, a UCLA teammate of Bill who became a renowned beach volleyball star . Vince Carter played both sports at Dayton Beach\u2019s Mainland High School, earning Conference Player of the Year status in 1994. Former NBA forward Chase Budinger was more of a standout at volleyball than basketball while at La Costa Canyon High in Carlsbad, Calif. During the 2011 lockout, Budinger joined his brother Duncan briefly on the pro beach tour. The offspring of numerous NBA figures, from Jermaine O\u2019Neal\u2019s daughter ( Asjia, committed to Texas ) to Houston coach Mike D\u2019Antoni\u2019s niece ( Bailey D\u2019Antoni, freshman at Marshall ), have snagged college volleyball scholarships. Another former NBA player, Jud Buechler, a member of Walton\u2019s staff, played volleyball in high school, then coached up his daughter, Reily, to a spot at UCLA .  Philadelphia\u2019s Joel Embiid was a 7-foot Cameroonian volleyball player before he got introduced to hoops shortly before an NBA Basketball Without Borders camp. And Portland coach Terry Stotts played high school volleyball in Guam when he attended high school there, his parents taking the family overseas in their jobs as teachers. \u201cVolleyball was a varsity sport, so I played volleyball for a couple years,\u201d Stotts said. \u201cThe things I would say transfer to basketball are the explosive jumping. Hand-eye coordination. Quick reflexes. Timing. Going to spike the ball is like going to get a rebound \u2014 you\u2019ve got to time your jump. Lateral quickness to the ball. So yeah, I would say there\u2019s some valid skills.\u201d So Stotts is OK if his rebounders occasionally tap out the ball rather than grabbing it. \u201cRobin Lopez used to do that for us on the offensive glass,\u201d the Blazers coach said, \u201cand we\u2019d get a lot of 3-pointers because of it.\u201d Said Poeltl: \u201cI actually do that a lot. I also find myself doing a lot of tip-ins. Maybe that has something to do with it.\u201d The 22-year-old\u2019s overall game has stepped up thanks largely to opportunity. Already, he has logged more minutes in 2017-18 than he did all of last season, his nightly shifts increasing by about 50 percent from 11.6 minutes to 17.8. His production has jumped accordingly \u2014 Poeltl is averaging 13.8 points and 9.5 rebounds per 36 minutes while shooting 63.7 percent . \u201cThe most important improvement I\u2019ve made was getting more comfortable on the court,\u201d said Poeltl, who is not afraid to challenge dunkers at the rim, regardless of the poster potential. \u201cJust gaining experience. I don\u2019t think it\u2019s anything I specifically worked on in my game.  \u201cThe chemistry with my teammates, finishing around the rim, all of that, small things have helped me. Knowing opponents, for sure. Knowing my own game more and more. How my teammates play and how I have to play around them.\u201d Said Toronto forward Pascal Siakam, Poeltl\u2019s best friend on the team after arriving as rookies together last season: \u201cI know he looks awkward, but he\u2019s doing a great job of moving his feet.\u201d Poeltl is still carrying that flag as the first Austrian drafted into the NBA, realizing a dream few others in his country had when the Raptors used the No. 9 pick on him in 2016 . Austria had a spirited basketball faction through the 1950s, with qualifying for EuroBasket competition six times. But it dropped off after that, with little or nothing to show in international competition over the past five decades. Poeltl\u2019s journey, however, has begun to revive basketball interest in his homeland, and he\u2019s just getting started. \u201cThat\u2019s what I\u2019m trying to do \u2014 be something of a role model for young basketball players in Austria,\u201d Poeltl said. \u201cI\u2019m really trying to make basketball more popular and get more kids to play. If I can have that kind of effect, that would be great.\u201d He is on the Austrian roster for the 2019 FIBA Basketball World Cup qualification (Europe), and participated in August in pre-qualifying games. Poeltl will remain strictly a one-sport participant, though, not crossing over to the one his parents played. \u201cThey know better,\u201d he said. \u201cI think [the national team organizers] have some better volleyball players than me.\u201d Volleyball\u2019s loss, the Raptors\u2019 and the NBA\u2019s gain. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Lillard need 441 games to reach the milestone. The previous franchise record was 500 games (Clyde Drexler). The memories from those title runs are forever the Rockets, but the actual hardware they won \u2014 the Larry O\u2019Brien Trophy \u2014 are in the possession of the man who owned the team back then. Leslie Alexander sold the Rockets for $2.2 billion to Houston businessman Tilman Fertitta early last fall , ending Alexander\u2019s 24-year stint as owner of the team. Houston claimed the 1993-94 NBA title after an epic season in which it won a franchise-record 58 games and topped the New York Knicks in Game 7 of The Finals. In 1994-95 , the Rockets became the first team to defeat four 50-win teams (Utah Jazz, Phoenix Suns, San Antonio Spurs and Orlando Magic) en route to sweeping the Magic in The Finals . Both of those championship runs came after Alexander bought the team in July of 1993. In an interview with David Barron of the Houston Chronicle , Alexander says he has the Rockets\u2019 two championship trophies at his house in Florida. His retaining of the trophies is permitted by the NBA : According to the Houston Chronicle , a Rockets spokesman says the team is in the process of ordering replica NBA trophies. Those trophies, once procured, will be displayed where the former trophies were \u2014 in the team\u2019s offices at Toyota Center and also at special events for Rockets season-ticket holders. DIFFICULTY: While 1.4 seconds seems like a short window to execute a play, in reality, that\u2019s an eternity. The Nuggets had plenty of time to get a shot off coming out of a timeout \u2014 the problem would be how clean of a look they would get. Without much difficulty, that problem was solved when Westbrook, who, as Nikola Jokic put it after the game, \u201cfell asleep\u201d on the final possession that left Harris with a wide-open look. GAME SITUATION: The Nuggets led 104-89 at the start of the fourth quarter. The lead began to dwindle as the frame went on and it was completely squandered when George pulled up for a huge step-back 3 that gave the All-Star 43 points and knotted things up for the first time since tipoff. Jokic, the team\u2019s best passer, was left to inbound the ball with 1.4 left and delivered a strike to the opposite wing. Harris came through with the cold-blooded trey. CELEBRATION: After seeing Westbrook and OKC display a classic celebration on the exact same court last season , it was exhilarating to see Harris and his teammates on the victorious side of a classic Northwest Division finish. GRADE: Three Horrys. Five shooting locations are positioned around the three-point arc. Four racks contain four NBA regulation balls (each worth one point) and one multicolored \u201cmoney\u201d ball (worth two points). The fifth rack is a special \u201call money ball\u201d rack, which each participant can place at any of the five locations. Every ball on this rack is worth two points. The players have one minute to shoot as many of the 25 balls as they can. The three competitors with the highest scores in the first round advance to the championship round. Here is a look at the four participants for the 2018 Verizon Slam Dunk: Victor Oladipo, Pacers: A first-time All-Star this season, Oladipo ranks 13th in the NBA in scoring (career-high 23.9 ppg) while shooting career highs of 48.8 percent from the field and 39.2 percent from three-point range. This is his second Verizon Slam Dunk appearance, having finished second to Zach LaVine in 2015. At the inaugural NBA Awards last June, Oladipo won the fan-voted Dunk of the Year for the 2016-17 season. The 6-4 guard is seeking to become the second consecutive Pacer to win the event; Indiana\u2019s Glenn Robinson III, the 2017 champion, is working his way back from ankle surgery.  Aaron Gordon, Magic: This marks the third consecutive Slam Dunk appearance for Gordon, whose runner-up finish to LaVine in 2016 included three straight perfect scores of 50. In his fourth season, the 6-9 forward is averaging career highs of 18.4 points and 8.3 rebounds. He has already set a career high for three-pointers made in a season with 79. Gordon has scored at least 40 points in a game twice this season \u2013 the first Magic player to do so since Dwight Howard in the 2010-11 season. Larry Nance Jr., Lakers: The 6-9 forward is the son of three-time NBA All-Star Larry Nance, who won the inaugural Verizon Slam Dunk in 1984 by defeating Julius Erving in the final round. Nance Jr. is posting career highs in scoring (8.8 ppg), rebounding (6.7 rpg) and field goal percentage (60.3) in his third season. He was a finalist for Dunk of the Year last season. Competing at his home arena, Nance is seeking to become the Lakers\u2019 first Slam Dunk winner since Kobe Bryant in 1997.  Dennis Smith Jr., Mavericks:  Renowned for his vertical leap , Smith ranks fifth among rookies in scoring (14.8 ppg) and fourth in assists (4.8 apg). The 6-3 guard registered his first triple-double on Dec. 29 against the New Orleans Pelicans, joining Jason Kidd (1994-95) as the only Dallas rookies with a triple-double. Smith, the ninth pick in NBA Draft 2017 presented by State Farm, will compete for the U.S. Team in Mtn Dew Kickstart Rising Stars on Friday, Feb. 16 during NBA All-Star 2018. Verizon Slam Dunk Rules In the two-round event, participants can perform any dunk they choose. The players have a maximum of three attempts to complete each dunk in both the first round and the final round. Five judges score every dunk on a scale of 6 to 10, resulting in a high score of 50 and a low score of 30. All four competitors get two dunks in the first round. The two players with the highest combined score for their two dunks advance to the head-to-head final round. The player with the highest combined score for his two dunks in the final round is crowned the champion.  Click here for a complete explanation of the rules.
